<leme no="1286">
<page sig="">
<section type="Title page">
AN
ENGLISH 
EXPOSITOR: 
TEACHING THE INTER&shy;
pretation
of the hardest words
used in our Language.

WITH SVNDRY EXPLICATIONS,
Descriptions, and Discourses.

By <f type="i">I. B.</f> Doctor of Physick.

<term lang="gk">[undefined span non-Latin alphabet]</term>

<f type="i">LONDON,</f> 
Printed by <f type="i">Iohn Legatt,</f> and are to be sold by 
<f type="i">Andrew Crooke,</f> in <f type="i">Pauls</f> Church-yard. 
1641.
</section>
</page>

<page sig="">
</page>

<page sig="">
<section type="Dedication">

<heading>TO THE RIGHT 
HONOVRABLE AND 
Vertuous, his singular good 
Lady, the Lady JANE Vicoun&shy;
tesse MOUNTAGUE, all ho&shy;
nour and happinesse.</heading>

BEing perswaded (Right Noble 
Lady) by some friends, for pub&shy;

like benefit to make this Colle&shy;
ction of words common, which 
at first was intended onely for 
private use, (as written in my youth; at the re&shy;
quest of a worthy Gentleman, one whose love
prevailed much with me) I could not finde in 
heart to send it forth, no better furnished than 
with a bare Title; lest like an unknown Infant, 
it would be exposed to over hard usage, or per&shy;
adventure scornfully rejected, adventuring a&shy;
broad without countenance of any friend or
</page>

<page sig="">
commander. Vpon sure knowledge therefore 
of your most honourable disposition, and former 
experience of your Ladiships favour toward 
me, I am emboldened to present this little 
Pamphlet unto your Honour, with hope that 
by your Patronage it shall not onely be protect&shy;
ed from injuries, but also finde favourable en&shy;
tertainment, and perhaps gracefully admitted
among greatest Ladies and studious Gentlewo&shy;
men, to whose reading (I am made beleeve) it 
will not prove altogether ungratefull. And al&shy;
though I may well be taxed of folly or pre&shy;
sumption, that for credit of a slender trifle, crave 
the assistance of so eminent a Person, yet such 
I confesse) is my care thereof (since I must not 
deny it for my own) and my dutie and devoti&shy;
on so affected to your Honour, and all yours;
that I have, (hoping of your Ladyships pardon) 
wilfully repelled all reasons which might dis&shy;
swade me, chusing herein not so much what 
may seem most seemly, as what I suppose most 
behovefull for me.

Accept therefore I beseech your Honour, 
and receive under your noble tuition this little 
Vocabulary Treatise, which having been many 
yeers restrained of libertie, is now glad of en&shy;
largement, especially recommended unto so 
worthy a Patronesse. The glorious Sun loseth
</page>

<page sig="">
not the least point of his heighth by liberall
lending down his light, and quickning with his 
influence the lowest creatures; neither is any 
eminency disgraced, that easily condescendeth
to others desired good: which is also confirmed
by the Poet, who saith;

<term lang="la">
Conspicitur nunquam meliore potentia causa,
Quam quoties vanas non sinit esse preces.
</term>

Not thinking needfull therefore to trouble 
your Honour with many words of entreatie
(upon assured confidence of your well known
Charitie and goodnesse) I conclude, sincerely
wishing to your Ladiship, the best that may be 
wished, and resting my self ever,


<f type="i">Your Honours to be 

commanded,</f>

JO. BULLOKAR.

</section>
</page>

<page sig="">
<section type="To the Courteous Reader">
<heading>To the Courteous Reader.</heading>

H<f type="i">Ere have you (Gentle Reader) that which 
at first was not made for you, though now 
willingly (for your benefit if you embrace 
it) offered to your kinde acceptance. Com&shy;
mend it my self I will not, lest I should give 
occasion to some quick heads, to come upon 
with the verse, <f type="i">Autor opus laudat, &amp;c.</f> Yet this I will 
say (and say truely) that in my younger yeers it hath cost me 
some observation, reading, study, and charge; which you may
easily beleeve, considering the great store of strange words, our 
speech doth borrow, not only from the Latine, and Greeke, (and 
some from the ancient Hebrew) but also from forraine vulgar 
Languages round about us: beside sundry old words now 
growne out of use, and divers termes of art, proper to the learn&shy;
ed in Logicke, Philosophy, Law, Physicke, Astronomie, &amp;c. 
yea, and Divinitie it self, best known to the severall profes&shy;
sors thereof. And herein I hope such learned will deeme no 
wrong offered to themselves or dishonour to Learning, in that 
I open the signification of such words; to the capacitie of the 
ignorant, whereby they may conceive and use them as well as 
those which have bestowed long study in the languages; for 
considering it is familiar among best writers to usurpe
strange words, (and sometime necessary by reason our speech
is not sufficiently furnished with apt termes to expresse all 
meanings) I suppose withall their desire is that they should 
also be understood; which I (knowing that</f> <f type="r">bonum qu&ograve; 
communius eo melius</f>) have endeavoured by this Booke,</f>
</page>

<page sig="">
though not exquisitely, yet (I trust) in some reasonable 
measure to performe. It is easier (all know) to finde faults,
then to mend them, and easier to mend faults in anothers 
worke already written, then to write any new work free from 
all fault. If therefore any fault finder, or over curious Cri&shy;
tick (for from the best learned I expect best usage) shall to 
shew his skill grow captious, and quarrell at my interpretati&shy;
ons, I will desire him to forbeare bitternesse, and temper a 
while his choler, till having laid this worke aside, he travell
himself in the same or some other new argument; and then I
doubt not but he will either become more indifferent, or give
others occasion to bid him English the old Proverbe,</f> <f type="r">Media
cura <emend err="teipsum">te ipsuim</emend>.</f> But as for you (judiciall or courteous Reader) 
whose favour I desire, and whose counsell or friendly correcti&shy;
on I will not refuse, if to you (I say) any thing herein shall
occurre, which seemeth by me omitted, mistaken, or not fully
satisfactory to your expectation, (for indeed there are almost
slipt away seven yeers since I had any leasure as much as to 
looke on it) I promise that upon warning  hereof given to me 
or the Printer, at a second Impression it shall be
amended or 
supplyed. Meanewhile use this as you finde it, and I perswade
my self your honesty will say it is worth the money you paid for 
it. So committing my self and Book to your favourable good
liking, I commit you to God.</f> <f type="r">From my house at</f> Chicester 
</f> <f type="r">in</f> Sussex,</f> <f type="r">this 17. day of</f> October. 1616.


Your heartie well-wisher, 
<f type="r">Jo: Bullokar.</f>

</section>
</page>

<page sig="">
<section type="Instruction to the Reader">
<heading>An Instruction to the Reader.</heading>

HAve care to search every word ac&shy;
cording to the true Orthography 
thereof, as for Ph&oelig;nix in the Letter
<f type="i">P.</f> not in <f type="i">F.</f> for Hypostaticall
in <f type="i">Hy:</f> not in 
<f type="i">Hi.</f> Remember also that every word mark&shy;
ed with this mark * is an old word, onely
used of some ancient Writers, and now 
grown out of use. Lastly, if a word be of 
different significations, the one easie, the o&shy;
ther more difficult, I onely speak of inter&shy;
pretation of the hardest; as in the words
<f type="i">Ten, Girle, Garter,</f> may appear.
</section>
</page>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Action.</form> <xpln lang="en">A deed done: 
or the doing of any thing. 
In our common Law it 
signifieth a suit commen&shy;
ced against any man, and 
is commonly divided into 
three differing kinds, 
viz, </xpln>

<subform lang="en">1. <f type="i">Action personall,</f></subform> <subxpln lang="en"> 
which is for debt, goods, 
cattell.</subxpln>

<subform lang="en">2. <f type="i">Action popular,</f></subform> <subxpln lang="en">which 
any man may sue, as upon 
the breach of a penall sta&shy;
tute, where some advan&shy;
tage is allowed to him that 
will sue for it.</subxpln>

<subform lang="en">3 <f type="i">Action reall,</f></subform> <subxpln lang="en">when one 
claimeth title to any lands 
tenements, rents, or com&shy;
mon, in fee simple, fee 
taile, or for terme of life.</subxpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Active.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Livelie, strong,

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Activitie.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Strong nim&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Adage.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A proverbe.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Adamant.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A precious 
stone commonly called a 
Diamond, brought out of 
Arabia and Cyprus. It is 
the hardest of all stones, 
insomuch that it cutteth 
glasse, and yeeldeth neither 
to stroke of hammer nor 
fire, for which cause the 
Greeks name it <f type="i">Adamas,</f> 
which in their tongue sig&shy;
nifieth Invincible: Not&shy;
withstanding it is softened 
with goats blood, being 
steeped therein new and 
warme. It is of contrarie 
nature to the loadstone, in 
so much that being laid 
near it, the loadstone can&shy;
not draw yron having the 
vertue thereof overmaste&shy;

</page>

<page sig="">
red by the Adamant. </xpln></wordentry>

give ones selfe much to 
any thing.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Additament.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Any thing

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Addition.</form> <xpln lang="en"> An adding or 
putting to. In our com&shy;
mon law it signifieth any 
title given to a man beside 
his name, which title shew&shy;
eth his estate, trade, course 
of life, and also dwelling 
place.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Adherence.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A cleaving

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Adjacent.</form> <xpln lang="en"> That which

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Adjourne.</form> <xpln lang="en">  To deferre

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Adjournement.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A terme 
in law when any Court is 
dissolved, and appointed 
to be kept upon some o&shy;
ther time, or at any other 
place.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Adjunct.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A qualitie, 
joyned to a thing, as 
heate to fire, coldnesse to 
Snow.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Adjure.</form> <xpln lang="en"> To binde 
by oath: to make one to 
sweare.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Africa.</form> <xpln lang="en"> One of three

is Barbary and all Ethiope 
contained. The people of 
these countries lived in 
times past very uncivilly, 
feeding much upon ser&shy;
pents flesh: It is called A&shy;
frica of the Greeke word 
<f type="i">Phrice,</f> which signifieth 
Cold: and the particle A. 
which in that language, 
being placed before a 
word, changeth the sense 
thereof: so that Africa 
signifieth a countrey hot 
or without cold.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Agarick.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A kind of mush&shy;

light, brittle, and sponge&shy;

fleame and grosse raw hu&shy;
mours out of the body, o&shy;
pening obstructions of the 
Liver, and by this meanes 
amendeth an evill colour.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Allegorie.</form> <xpln lang="en">A sentence

<f type="i">Matt.</f> 3. said: <f type="i">Whose fanne

</page>

<page sig="">
make cleane his floore, and ga&shy;

The meaning whereof is, 
that Christ being su&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Allelujah.</form> <xpln lang="en"> An Hebrew word

and is interpreted, Praise 
ye our Lord. <f type="i">Paulus Diaco&shy;
nus</f> writeth, that when the 
Britains were invaded by

on a time readie to fight a

(who was sent hither with 
Saint <f type="i">Lupus</f> to confute the 
<f type="i">Pelagian</f> heresy) that they 
should doe as he did; and 
forth with he cryed aloud 
<f type="i">Alleluja:</f> which when the 
whole army of Britains 
had done, the sound there&shy;
of strooke such a terrour 
into the enemies, that 
they presently fled away, 
where the Britains had 
the victory, <f type="i">De gestis Rom. 
lib.</f> 15.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="'or(n)">Aloes, <f type="r">or</f> Lignum Aloes.</form> <xpln lang="en">
A precious wood used in

eth the heart, and openeth

</page>

<page sig="">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Aloesuccotrina.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The juyce

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Amazon.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A woman of 
the Country Amazonica. 
Amazones were warlike 
women of Scythia, which 
kept a Countrey to them&shy;
selves without men, yet to 
have children companied 
with the bordering people. 
Their sonnes they either 
destroyed or sent home to 
the father, but their daugh&shy;
ters they kept, bringing 
them up in hunting, ti&shy;
ding, shooting and feats 
of armes. They burned 
the right breast of their 
children, lest it should hin&shy;
der their archerie, where&shy;
fore they had the name 
Amazons, which (in 
Greeke) signifieth women 
wanting a breast.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Amber.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A kinde of hard

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Ambergrise.</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="i">Mesue</f> saith

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Aspe.</form> <xpln lang="en">A venemous Ser&shy;
pent of a blacke earthie 
colour, and sometime yel&shy;
low. The superstitious E&shy;
gyptians did honour them, 
and their Kings used to 
weare the figure of an Asp 
in their Diadems. They go 
alwaies two and two toge&shy;
ther, and if it happen that 
one of them be killed, the 
other will presently pursue 
him that slew his fellow, in 
such sort that he shall hard&shy;
ly escape, unlesse he make 
great hast or passe over a 
river. If one be bitten by 
this Serpent, the best re&shy;
medie is presently to cut 
off the members so bitten, 
if it be possible; otherwise 
he shall fall into a deadly 
sleep, &amp; within few houres 
goe away, as it were in a 
trance. <f type="i">Cleopatra</f> Queene 
of Egypt, after the death 
of <f type="i">Marcus Antonius</f>  (whom  
</page>

<page sig="">
she loved as her Husband) 
slue her selfe wilfully, by 
applying one of these aspes 
to her body; because she 
would not be carryed in 
triumph to Rome, by <f type="i">Au&shy;
gustus Caesar,</f> who had van&shy;
quished her and <f type="i">Antonie.</f> </xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Aspect&#0183;</form> <xpln lang="en"> Sight or the be&shy;
holding of any thing. In 
Astronomie it signifieth 
the distance betweene the 
planets &amp; heavenly signes: 
And there are foure such 
Aspects. The first called a 
Trine aspect (because it 
divideth the heavens into 
three even parts) is the 
distance of foure signes 
from each other; as <f type="i">Aries</f>  
beholdeth <f type="i">Leo</f> and <f type="i">Sagit&shy;
tarius</f> with a Trine aspect, 
because these are distant 
foure signes, the one be&shy;
fore, the other after <f type="i">Aries.</f> 
The second called a Quar&shy;
till, is the distance of three 
signes, as <f type="i">Aries</f> beholdeth 
<f type="i">Cancer</f> and <f type="i">Capricorne,</f>  with 
a Quartill aspect, because 
they are distant three 
signes from him. The 
third called a Sextill as&shy;
pect is the distance of two 
signes, as <f type="i">Aries</f> beholdeth 
<f type="i">Gemini</f> and <f type="i">Aquarius</f> with 
this Sextill aspect, being 
but two signes distant 
from them. The fourth 
called an Opposite aspect, 
is the farthest distance that 
can be, namely a distance 
of six signes asunder; as 
<f type="i">Aries</f> beholdeth <f type="i">Libra</f> 
with this opposite aspect, 
and <f type="i">Libra</f>  beholdeth <f type="i">Aries</f> 
with the same. The like is 
of all the other signes, or 
Planets placed in them. 
For example; <f type="i">Taurus</f> be&shy;
holdeth <f type="i">Cancer</f> and <f type="i">Pisces</f> 
with a Sextill, <f type="i">Leo</f> and <f type="i">A&shy;
quarius</f>  with a Quartill, 
<f type="i">Virgo</f> and <f type="i">Capricorne</f> with a 
Trine, and <f type="i">Scorpio</f> with an 
opposite aspect. The di&shy;
stance of one or five signes 
is not called an aspect.</xpln></wordentry>

</page>

<page sig="">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Balme.</form> <xpln lang="en">A precious juice 
or liquor, otherwise called 
<f type="i">Balsamum,</f> or <f type="i">Opobalsa&shy;
mum.</f>  It droppeth by cut&shy;
ting out of a little low 
plant (about a yard high) 
having leaves like Rue, but 
whiter, which plant grow&shy;
eth in Egypt, and some 
places of the holy Land. 
This juice is somewhat 
like to oyle, but more 
clammie, and inclining to 
a certaine rednesse. It hath 
a strong smell, and is not 
pleasant in taste: Being 
put into a vessell of water, 
it will sinke downe to the 
bottome like a round 
pearle, without breaking, 
and may be taken up a&shy;
gaine with the point of a 
knife. It is an excellent 
medicine to take any skar 
out of the body, and for 
divers other purposes, but very costly and rarely got&shy;
</page>

<page sig="">
ten.  <f type="i">Saladinus</f> writes that 
there was but one vine&shy;
yard of these in the whole

began to Baptize or wash 
men in the River Jordan 
to the remission of sins.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Barbarisme.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Rudenesse in

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Basiliske.</form> <xpln lang="en">Otherwise cal&shy;
led a Cockatrise: the most 
venemous serpent that is. 
It breaketh stones and 
blasteth all plants with the 
breath thereof, burning e&shy;
very thing that it goeth o&shy;
ver; neither can any herbe 
growe neere the place 
where it lyeth. It is poy&shy;
son to poyson, and driveth 
away all other serpents, 
with only hissing. If a man 
touch it but with a sticke, 
it will kill him, and if 
it see a man a farre off, it 
destroyeth him with his 
looks. This serpent is not 
above a foot long, of color 
between black &amp; yellow, 
having red eyes, a very 
sharpe head, and a white  
</page>

<page sig="">
spot thereon like a crown 
wherefore he is called by 
some in Latine <f type="i">Regulus,</f> 
viz. a little king. It goeth 
not winding like other ser&shy;
pents, but upright unto the 
middle, holding up the 
brest thereof. It breedeth 
onely in the hot burning 
sands of Africa: Of this 
Basiliske the Poet <f type="i">Lucane</f> 
writeth thus. <f type="i">lib.</f> 9. 

Sibilaque effundens cunct as terrentia pestes. 
Ante venena nocens, late sibi submovet omne 
Vulgus, &amp; in vacua regnat Basiliscus arena. 
With deadly hiss the Basiliske, 
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;all other plagues doth fright. 
And speedier kils then poisons can, 
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;with his infectious sight. 
Hee'le have no neighbour dwell neer him, 
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;he loves to live alone. 
And tyrantlike reignes by himself, 
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;in cave of sandy stone.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Bassae.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A noble man, or 
great Commander under 
the great Turke.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="'of(n)">Beades of Saint Elline.</form> <xpln lang="en">Cer&shy;

</page>

<page sig="">
are of great vertue against 
griefs of the stomack, as al&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Beaver.</form> <xpln lang="en"> In armour it sig&shy;
nifieth that part of the hel&shy;
met which may be lifted 
up, to take breath the more 
freely: It is also a beast of 
very hot nature, living 
much in the water. His 
two forefeet are like the 
feet of the beast called 
<f type="i">Gattus,</f> (as <f type="i">Joannes de San&shy;
cto Amando</f> writeth:) but 
what this <f type="i">Gattus</f> is, I doe 
not well understand, only 
I suppose it to be an <f type="i">Ottter.</f> 
Vpon these feet he hath 
very sharp claws, where&shy;
with he taketh his prey, 
and hangeth upon water 
banks. His hinder feet are 
made like to the foot of a 
Goose, wherewith hee 
swimeth. He hath very 
sharpe teeth, and doth 
much harme to trees, with 
knawing the roots and 
rynds of them. It is writ&shy;
ten that if a Beaver come 
into a strange place, where 
he hath not been bred, 
the other Beavers will 
make him their slave, to 
provide them meat, and 
pull off all the haire from 
his backe that he may be 
known. The stones of 
this beast are sold in Apo&shy;
thecaries shops, by the 
name of <f type="i">Castoreum:</f> they 
are much used in Physick, 
being very good against 
palsies and cold diseases of 
the sinews: But the skin 
is of more valew then the 
stones.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Beeglue.</form> <xpln lang="en"> That which

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Beestings.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The first milke

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Berill.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A precious stone

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Beza stone.</form> <xpln lang="en">A stone of 
excellent vertue against 
poyson, very costly and of 
great account in Physick. 
It is commonly of the 
bignesse of an Acorne or 
Chestnut, being compoun&shy;
ded of certain thin scales, 
one upon another, like 
the scales of an Onion. It 
is easie to be scraped or 
cut, and if it continue 
long in water, it melteth. 
The middle part is some&shy;
thing hollow, and full of  
</page>

<page sig="">
powder of the same sub&shy;
stance that the stone is of. 
And this is a marke to 
know whether the stone 
be fine and true: for the 
Indians doe counterfeit 
them sometimes and de&shy;
ceive many. This stone 
is taken out of the bow&shy;
els of a beast in India, 
much like a Hart, saving 
that his hornes are like 
a goates. The occasion 
of the growing of the 
stones (as some write) is 
thus. These Harts or wilde 
Goats (for they resem&shy;
ble both) going to the 
dennes of Serpents in 
those countries, doe with 
their breathing compell 
them to come forth, and 
then eate them: after 
they goe whereas water 
is, and plunge themselves 
therein untill they perceive 
the furie of the venome 
be past, and till then they 
will not drinke a drop: 
being come forth of 
the water, they goe into 
the fields, where feed&shy;
ing upon many healthfull 
hearbs (known to them 
by naturall instinct to be 
of vertue against poyson) 
they are perfectly freed 
from all danger; and by 
the mixture of these herbs 
with the Serpents eaten 
before, these Bezar stones 
are very strangely (as is 
said) ingendered within 
them: growing by little 
and little, as appeareth 
by the scales thereof one 
folded upon another. </xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Bezill.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The broad part 
of a Ring, in which the 
stone or signet is set.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Bissextile.</form> <xpln lang="en">Leape yeere,

Calends of March are in  
</page>

<page sig="">
that yeere twice reckoned: 
viz. on the 24. and 25. of 
Februarie, so that leape 
yeere hath one day more 
than other yeeres have. 
This leape yeere is obser&shy;
ved every fourth yeere, and 
was first devised by <f type="i">Julius 
Caesar,</f> to accommodate or 
make the yeere agree with 
the course of the Sun. </xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Bitumen.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A kind of clay, 
naturally clamy like pitch; 
growing in some coun&shy;
tries of Asia. It was of old 
used in Physick: The best 
is heavy, bright and cleere, 
of purple colour, and ha&shy;
ving a strong smell. The 
black is accounted naught. 
This <f type="i">Bitumen</f> was used in 
stead of morter, at the 
building of the tower of 
Babel, as appeareth in <f type="i">Gen.</f> 
cap. 11. There is also a 
kind of <f type="i">Bitumen,</f> like unto 
a liquor, flowing out of 
some fountains in the I&shy;
land Sicilie, which is used 
in stead of oyle to burne 
in lamps.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Calends.</form> <xpln lang="en"> It signifieth pro&shy;
perly, the first day of every 
moneth, being spoken a&shy;
lone by it self. If <f type="i">Pridie</f> be 
placed before it, then it 
signifieth the last day of 
the moneth going before, 
as <f type="i">Pridie, Calend. Ianuarii.</f> 
is the last day of Decem&shy;
ber. If any number be 
placed with it, it signifieth 
that day in the former 
moneth, which cometh so 
much before the moneth 
named; as the tenth Ca&shy;
lends of March is the 
twentieth day of Februa&shy;
ry, because if one begin at 
March, and reckon back&shy;
wards, that twentieth day 
is the tenth day before 
March. In March, May, 
July, and October, the 
Calends beginne at the 
sixteenth day, in other 
moneths at the fourteenth 
which Calends must ever 
beare the name of the 
moneth following, and be 
numbred backward from 
the first day of the said 
following moneths.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Canker.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A hard swelling 
in the veines, being over&shy;
charged with hot melan&shy;
choly humors. It is cal&shy;
led a Canker, because the 
veines so swollen are like 
unto the clawes of a Crab. 
This disease may happen 
in any part of the body, 
but most commonly in 
womens breasts; by reason 
of their spongie hollow&shy;
nesse, and great number of 
veines there meeting. If 
it continue long it is very 
hard to cure, because gen&shy;
tle medicines will doe no 
good, and strong doe in&shy;
crease the raging malice 
of it.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Carbuncle.</form> <xpln lang="en">It hath two 
significations, namely a

gerous sore. 1. Carbun&shy;
cle stone, is bright, of the 
colour of fire. It hath ma&shy;
ny vertues, but chiefely 
prevaileth against the dan&shy;
ger of infectious aire. The 
best of these stones will

a little, and are lesse estee&shy;
med; but such as shine not  
</page>

<page sig="">
at all, are scarce of any rec&shy;
koning: these stones are 
found in some countreys 
of Africa. 2. Carbuncle 
disease, is a botch or ulcer, 
(otherwise called by a 
Greeke name <f type="i"><term lang="gk">Anthrax</term></f>) 
caused of grosse hot blood, 
which raiseth blisters, and 
burneth the skin: This 
ulcer is ever accompanied 
with a Feaver. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Centaures.</form> <xpln lang="en"> People of

made in the likenesse of 
<f type="i">Iuno.</f> They warred on the 
Lapithes, and were over&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Center.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The point in the

a globe. Wherefore the

of the world, because it is 
in the midst thereof.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Charles Wayne.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Certaine

in fashion like foure 
wheeles and horses draw&shy;

listo,</f> King <f type="i">Lycaons</f> daugh&shy;

<f type="i">Jupiter,</f> was by <f type="i">Juno</f> in de&shy;

<f type="i">Calisto</f> afterward, into this

<f type="i">Arctos,</f> which signifieth a

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Cinoper.</form> <xpln lang="en">A soft red stone,

nims did use to paint their

selves in publike feasts and 
solemnities, as we reade 
that <f type="i">Camillus</f> when he tri&shy;

painted with this Vermili&shy;
on: So <f type="i">Virgill</f>  speaking in 
his tenth <f type="i">Eclog,</f> of the shep&shy;
herds God <f type="i">Pan,</f> saith that 
he was seene, 

<f type="i">Sanguineis chuli baccis minioque rubentem.</f> 
With bloody Walwort berries stayn'd, and with Vermilion red. 
</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Climactericall.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A Greeke 
word signifying an account 
or reckoning, made by cer&shy;
taine degrees or steps. 
Some have hereby divi&shy;
ded the age of mans life 
after this manner. The 
seventh yeere they reckon 
for dangerous; and by this 
account the 14.21.28.
35. &amp;c. are climactericall 
yeers. Likewise the ninth 
yeere is esteemed equally 
as dangerous, and by this 
account, the 18.27.36. 
&amp;c. are called Climacteri&shy;
call yeeres. But the most 
noted and famous Clima&shy;
ctericall yeere, is at the 
age of 63. because both ac&shy;
counts doe meete in this 
number; namely 7. times 
9. and 9. times 7. And this 
is held the most dangerous 
yeere of all other.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Collaterall.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Sidewaies, or

<f type="i">Collaterall</f> is that which

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Coloquintida.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A kinde of 
wilde Gourd, which the 
Persians name Gall of 
the earth, because it de&shy;
stroyeth all hearbs, neere 
which it groweth. It is 
round, having a thinne 
yellow rinde (when it is 
ripe) and the inner part 
open, and spongie full of 
gray seed. If there grow 
but one upon a plant, it 
must be throwne away, 
because it will be too vio&shy;
lent in operation. This 
fruit is often used in phy&shy;
sick to purge slimie grosse 
humours from the sinews 
and joynts; but must be 
well prepared, for that o&shy;
therwise it is very dange&shy;
rous.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Criticall.</form> <xpln lang="en">In Physicke the 
fourth and seventh dayes 
are called Critical, because 
in them Physitians use to 
judge of the danger of a 
disease: But the seventh 
is accounted the chiefe 
Criticall day, &amp; the fourth 
a token or signe what the 
seventh day will be, if the 
Patient live so long. This 
account must be made 
according to the number 
of weeks thus. In the first 
weeke the fourth day is 
the token or Critical of the  
</page>

<page sig="">
seventh day. In the second 
weeke the eleventh is the 
Critical of the fourteenth. 
In the third the seven&shy;
teenth is the Criticall of 
the twentieth: for <f type="i">Hippo&shy;
crates</f>  reckoneth the twen&shy;
tieth day for the last of the 
third weeke: In the fourth 
weeke 24. is the Criticall 
of the 27 In the fift, the 
31. the Criticall of the 
34. In the sixt weeke the 
37. the Criticall of the 
40. And so forth to a 
hundred. </xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Criticke.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The same that 
Criticall is. Also it signi&shy;
fieth sometime, one that 
hath authoritie, or taketh 
upon him to censure other 
mens acts or works writ<expan type="e_">en</expan>.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Crocrodile.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A harmefull 
beast living most about 
the River <f type="i">Nilus</f> in Egypt. 
It is hatched of an egge, 
and groweth unto a won&shy;
derfull greatnesse, some&shy;
time to twentie or thirtie 
foot long. This beast hath 
no tongue, and in feeding 
moveth onely the upper 
jawe: He hath eyes like a 
Hogge, and cruell sharpe 
teeth: He hath no haire, 
but is made much after 
the fashion of an Evet, 
with a long taile, a long 
belly, and backe covered 
all over with scales close 
joyned &amp; of great strength. 
Vpon his feete he hath 
strong sharpe clawes. In 
the day time hee can see 
farre, and liveth on land, 
in the night he is almost 
blind, and keepeth in the 
water. He is very bold up&shy;
on those he seeth afraid of 
him, and feare full if he be 
assaulted. It is written that 
he will weepe over a mans 
head, when he hath devou&shy;
red the body, and then will 
eate up the head too. 
Wherefore in Latine there 
is a proverbe, <f type="i">Crocodili La&shy;
chrym&aelig;,</f> Crocodiles teares, 
to signifie such teares as 
are fained, and spent onely 
with intent to deceive, or 
do harme. I saw once one 
of these beasts in London 
brought thither dead, but 
in perfect forme, of about 
three yards long.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Cynike.</form> <xpln lang="en">  Doggish or cur&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Degree.</form> <xpln lang="en">A terme often 
used in Astronomie and 
Physick. In Astronomie it 
signifieth the thirtieth part 
of a signe: viz. of <f type="i">Aries, 
Taurus, Gemini, &amp;c.</f> For in&shy;
to so many parts or de&shy;
grees are all the signes di&shy;
vided. In Physick it sig&shy;
  
</page>

<page sig="">
nifieth a proportion of 
heat, cold, moisture or dri&shy;
nesse, in the nature of 
simples; and there are 
foure such proportions or 
degrees: The first degree 
is so smal, that it can scarce 
be perceived. The second, 
that which may be mani&shy;
festly perceived without 
hurting the sense. The 
third, that which some&shy;
what offendeth the sense. 
The fourth, which so 
much offendeth, that it 
may destroy the body. For 
example: Sweet Almonds, 
Rice, Buglosse, ripe Grapes 
are hot in the first degree: Parsley, Saffron, Honey, in the second degree; Cum&shy;
mine, Galingall, Pepper 
in the third degree: and 
Garlike, Spourge, Euphor&shy;
bium, in the fourth degree. 
So Barly is cold in the first 
degree, Cucumbers in the 
second, Sengreene in the 
third, and Hemlocke in 
the fourth degree. Where 
note that in heat, cold, and 
drinesse, there may be 
foure degrees, and in moi&shy;
sture but two. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Diagridium.</form> <xpln lang="en"> There is

<page sig="">

thens: 2. In Jonia: 3. In

5. that manner of speech

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Dictator.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A chiefe Offi&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Divination.</form> <xpln lang="en">A fore&shy;
telling of a thing before 
it happeneth: which may 
(as I take it) be divi&shy;
ded into three different 
kinds; namely, super&shy;
naturall, naturall, and su&shy;
perstitious Divination. 
Supernaturall Divination  
</page>

<page sig="">
(onely revealed to man 
by God,) is not properly 
called. Divination, but 
prophecie, with which all 
the holy Prophets have in 
former times been inspi&shy;
red. Naturall Divination 
may be divided into two 
branches; whereof the 
first is, that which hath 
in former times been pra&shy;
ctised by wicked spirits in 
oracles and answers given 
by them in Idols, and is 
at this day sometime seene 
in possessed persons, who 
by suggestion of the devill 
may foretell often things 
to come: and this is but a 
naturall Divination: For 
although to us it seeme 
miraculous, because of our 
ignorance in the causes 
and course of things, yet in 
those spirits, it is but natu&shy;
rall, who by their long ex&shy;
perience, and great obser&shy;
vation, beside the know&shy;
ledge of secrets in nature, 
and their quicke intelli&shy;
gence from all places, are 
able to foresee much more 
then we by nature can. 
The second branch of na&shy;
turall Divination, is that 
which a wise man may 
foretell by probable con&shy;
jecture, being no way of&shy;
fensive, so long as it is on&shy;
ly guided by reason, and 
overruled by submitting 
it selfe to the Almightie 
power of GOD. And 
to this second kind of 
Divination, may also A&shy;
strologie bee referred 
(which by the motion 
and influence of Starres 
and Planets doth pro&shy;
mise to foretell many 
things) so long as it kee&shy;
peth it selfe in due limits, 
and arrogateth not too 
much to the certaintie 
thereof: into which ex&shy;
cesse of vanitie if it should 
breake forth, it can then 
be no longer called na&shy;
turall Divination, but 
superstitious and wicked: 
for the Starres may in&shy;
cline, but not impose a 
necessitie in particular 
things. The third and 
last manner of Divinati&shy;
on, is that which we cal&shy;
led superstitious, where&shy;
of there hath among the 
Gentiles been divers dif&shy;
ferent kinds, namtly, <f type="i">Au&shy;

</page>

<page sig="">
guration.  Aruspicie, Necro&shy;
mancie, Geomancie, Hydro&shy;
mancie, Pyromancie, Cosci&shy;
nomancie, Palmistrie</f> or <f type="i">Chi&shy;
romancie.</f>  
</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Auguration,</form> <xpln lang="en">was a divi&shy;
nation made of things to 
come, by the flying, fee&shy;
ding, and chirping of birds: 
the professors whereof cal&shy;
led <f type="i">Augurs,</f> were of great 
account among the heath<expan type="e_">en</expan> 
Romans, in so much that 
there was a Colledge of 
them in the City; neither 
would the Romans under&shy;
take any publike matter of 
import<expan type="a_">an</expan>ce, without asking 
their assent. But the vanity 
hereof was well derided by 
a wise Jew, named <f type="i">Mosso&shy;
lamus;</f>  as <f type="i">Josephus</f> writes. 
For an <f type="i">Augur</f> in the wars 
once requiring, that the 
army which was then mar&shy;
ching, might stand still a 
while, till he tooke obser&shy;
vation of a bird there by, 
to foreknow the successe of 
that expedition, this Jew 
whilst the <f type="i">Augur</f> was bu&shy;
sie in his art, shot at the 
Bird with an arrow, but 
by chance killed her: 
whereat the <f type="i">Augur</f> and 
others being highly offen&shy;
ded: Are you so foolish 
(quoth the Jew) to ima&shy;
gine this poore Bird can 
tell what will happen to 
us, that could not foresee 
her owne death so neere 
at hand? <f type="i">Aruspicie</f> is a 
divination, which by ope&shy;
ning and viewing the bow&shy;
els of beasts, did under&shy;
take to foretell things to 
come: the professors 
whereof were called <f type="i">A&shy;
ruspices. Necromancie,</f>  the 
worst of all others, is 
that divination, which is 
practised by conjuration, 
and calling up Devils or 
dead mens Ghosts. Which 
manner of divination we 
read practised by King 
<f type="i">Saul</f> (1. <f type="i">Reg. cap.</f> 28.) 
when he required a Sor&shy;
ceresse to call up the spirit 
of <f type="i">Samuel</f> to him. <f type="i">Geo&shy;
mancie,</f> is a kind of divina&shy;
tion practised by making 
prickes and lines in the 
earth; as the name in 
Greeke signifieth. So <f type="i">Hy&shy;
dromancie,</f>  is a divination 
made by some apparition 
in water, as <f type="i">Varro</f> writeth, 
that a Boy saw in water,  
</page>

<page sig="">
one bearing the forme of 
<f type="i">Mercury,</f> who foretold in 
one hundred and fiftie 
verses, the event of the 
warre which the Ro&shy;
manes had with King 
<f type="i">Mithridates. Pyromancie</f> 
is a divination made by 
the fire, or spirits ap&shy;
pearing in the fire. <f type="i">Cos&shy;
tinomancie</f>  is a ridiculous 
kind of divination made 
with a sieve; which at this 
day is used by some sim&shy;
ple women, and appea&shy;
reth to be of antiquitie, 
for in the third Idylle of 
<f type="i">Theocritus</f> there is men&shy;
tion made hereof. <f type="i">Palmi&shy;
strie</f> or <f type="i">Chiromancie,</f> is 
a divination practised, by 
looking upon the lines of 
the fingers and hands, 
an art still in use, among 
fortune tellers, Egypti&shy;
ans, and Juglers. Besides 
these there were also o&shy;
ther divinations, as name&shy;
ly <f type="i">Acromancie,</f> that which 
is gathered by apparitions 
in the aire. <f type="i">Capnomancie,</f> 
by the flying of smoake. 
<f type="i">Catoptromancie</f> by visions 
shewne in a glasse. All 
which being even by the 
Pagans themselves ac&shy;
counted deceitfull and 
vaine, it remaineth that of 
Christians they be utterly 
rejected and abhorred.  </xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Divorce.</form> <xpln lang="en">A separation of 
man and wife, which was 
(as our Saviour witnes&shy;
seth) first permitted by 
<f type="i">Moses</f> unto the Israelites, 
for the hardnesse of their 
hearts, that men might 
rather put their wives a&shy;
way, whom they grew 
wearie of, than use them 
with too great extremitie, 
to shorten their lives, as 
many did. The woman so 
divorced was to have of 
her husband, a writing (as 
<f type="i">Josephus</f> witnesseth) to this 
effect. I promise, that here&shy;
after, I will lay no claime 
to thee: And this writing 
was called a bil of divorce. 
But with Christians this custome is abrogated, sa&shy;
ving onely in case of a&shy;
dulterie. The ancient 
Romanes also had a cu&shy;
stome of divorce, and a&shy;
mongst them, it was as 
lawfull for the wives to 
put away their husbands, 
as for the husband to dis&shy;
 
</page>

<page sig="">
misse his wife: but amongst 
the Israelites, this preroga&shy;
tive was onely permitted 
to the husband. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Eclipse.</form> <xpln lang="en">A fayling or want of any thing: Com&shy;

</page>

<page sig="">
monly it signifieth a want 
of light; and there be two 
such Eclipses, namely of 
the Moone and of the 
Sunne. Eclipse of the 
Moone; never happeneth 
but at the full Moone, nei&shy;
ther then alwaies, but wh<expan type="e_">en</expan> she is in such a point, that 
the shadow of the earth de&shy;
priveth her of the Sunne 
beames, from whence she 
taketh her light, Eclipse of 
the Sunne is not so usuall, 
and happeneth only at the 
change of the Moone, 
namely when the Moone 
being between the Sunne 
and us, doth with her dark 
body, hide part of her light 
from us: which was the 
cause that <f type="i">Dionysius Areopa&shy;
gita,</f> seeing the Sunne 
so admirably eclipsed at 
our Saviours Passion, con&shy;
trarie to all reason, when 
the Moon was not in any 
neerenesse to hinder his 
light, cryed out in amaze&shy;
ment: <f type="i"><term lang="la">Aut Deus natur&aelig; 
patitur, aut machina mun&shy;
di dissolvetur</term>.</f> Either the 
God of nature suffereth, 
or else the frame of the 
world will be destroyed. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Enthymeme.</form> <xpln lang="en">A terme of Logick. It signifieth an im&shy;
perfect syllogisme, which wanteth either the Major or Minor: as for example. 

Every sin deserveth cor&shy;
rection.

Every theft is a sin.

Therefore every theft deserveth correction.

Now if we will leave  
</page>

<page sig="">
the first part (called the <f type="i">Major</f>) and say thus: <f type="i">Every theft is a sinne:</f>  Therefore <f type="i">Every theft deserveth cor&shy;
rection.</f> Or omit the second part (named the <f type="i">Minor</f>) and say <f type="i">Every sinne deser&shy;
veth correction,</f> therefore every <f type="i">theft deserveth cor&shy;
rection,</f> Then it is called an <f type="i">Enthymeme,</f> to wit, a keeping in the mind (for so the word properly sig&shy;
nifieth) because one of these parts is understood in the mind: where note that if the two endes of the Enthymeme are like in speech, then the Minor is wanting, if the two beinnings be like, the Major is omitted, as may easily appeare by the fore&shy;
going example. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Enveloped.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Wrapped.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Enumeration.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A recko&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Enunciative.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Any thing pronounced or spoken. A proposition or speech, w<SUP>ch</SUP> simply affirmed or denyeth any thing; as <f type="i">Cicero is ver&shy;
tuous: Cicero is not vertuous.</f></xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Epact.</form> <xpln lang="en">A number which is in use every yeer, to find the age of the Moone by: This Epact changeth yeer&shy;
ly, and is made by the ad&shy;
dition of eleven to the for&shy;
mer Epact; both which numbers are the Epact for the following yeer, but al&shy;
waies so, that both those numbers exceed not the number of 30. For if they amount to above 30. the<img src="/images/illeg_lett_single.gif" width="5" height="9" border="0" alt="Single illegible letter" title="Single illegible letter"> must you cast away the 30. and the remaining number shall be the Epact. As for example, in this yeere 1616. the Epact is 22. to which if you adde eleven for the next yeers Epact, it maketh 33. from which if you take away 30. there will remaine three; which is the Epact for the next yeere 1617. Note also: when in any yeere the E&shy;
pact is 29. you must ad 12. to find out the next yeeres true Epact, that casting a&shy;
way thirty, the Epact may come to be eleven. In al o&shy;
ther numbers the Epact is still made (as before) by  
</page>

<page sig="">
eleven. And these Epacts are ever changed on the first day of March. </xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Ephemerides.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A booke wherein day acts are regi&shy;
stred. Commonly it is ta&shy;
ken for a Booke of Astro&shy;
nomie (in use among such as erect figures to cast mens nativities) by which booke is shewne how all the Planets are placed, everie day and houre of the yeare.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Ephi.</form> <xpln lang="en"> An Hebrew mea&shy;
sure containing about five pecks of ours.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Ephod.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A holy garment worne by the high Priest of the Hebrewes, when he executed his function. It covered the back, and was curiouslie wrought with gold and twisted silke of Purple, Skarlet, and Vio&shy;
let colour. On the shoul&shy;
ders there were set two great precious Onix stones, and in them graven the names of the twelve sonnes of <f type="i">Iacob,</f> called the twelve Patriarchs, in the right shoulder the six el&shy;
dest, and in the left the six youngest; that the High Priest entring into the <f type="i">Sanctum Sanctorum</f> (which was the holiest place of the Temple) might beare with him the names of the peo&shy;
ple, for whom he was to pray unto God.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Epicycle.</form> <xpln lang="en">A terme used in Astronomie. It signifieth a lesser circle, whose cen&shy;
ter or middle part is in the circumference of a greater circle. In the upper part of this E<img src="/images/illeg_lett_single.gif" width="5" height="9" border="0" alt="Single illegible letter" title="Single illegible letter">icycle, the five Pla&shy;
nets, <f type="i">Saturne, Jupiter, Mars, Venus,</f> and <f type="i">Mercury,</f> doe goe forward according to the course of the signes; as <f type="i">Aries</f> to <f type="i">Taurus,</f> &amp;c. in the lower part they are re&shy;
trograde, that is, goe back&shy;
ward, as from <f type="i">Gemini</f> to <f type="i">Taurus,</f> from <f type="i">Taurus</f> to <f type="i">Aries</f>  againe. Betweene these two motions, are said to be two stations; namely when a Planet ceaseth going forward, and beginneth to be retro&shy;
grade, or comming to the farthest point of his retro&shy;
gradation, goeth forward againe: so that in the Epi&shy;
cycle, these Planets wheele about somtime according,  
</page>

<page sig="">
sometime contrary to the order of the signs. </xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Epicure.</form> <xpln lang="en"> It is commonly <img src="/images/illeg_lett_single.gif" width="5" height="9" border="0" alt="Single illegible letter" title="Single illegible letter">aken with us, for a man given over much to plea&shy;
sure, especially in gluttony. In ancient times it signi&shy;
fied one that followed the sect of a Philosopher na&shy;
med <f type="i">Epicurus,</f> who taught that the greatest happines was, to be without paine, and enjoy pleasure of bo&shy;
die and mind.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Epilepsie.</form> <xpln lang="en">The falling sicknesse, whereto most commonly children and yong folkare subject. This disease is caused by some humor or vapor, suddenly stopping the passage of spi&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Euphorbium.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A gum or teare of a strange plant, growing on the mount Atlas in Lybia. It is yello&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Fire-drake.</form> <xpln lang="en">A fire some&shy;

</page>

<page sig="">
night like a Dragon. Common people thinke it a spirit, that keepeth some treasure hid: but Philosophers affirme it to be a great unequall exha&shy;
lation; inflamed between two Cloudes, the one hot, the other cold (which is the reason that it also smo&shy;
keth:) the middle part whereof, according to the proportion of the hote Cloud, being greater then the rest, maketh it seeme like a bellie, and both ends like unto a head and taile. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Fistike nuts.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Outlandish nuts, brought out of Syria and other hot Countries, not much unlike a small Hasell nut. bled with shortnesse of breath, and are comforta&shy;
ble for the stomacke; yet they nourish but little, be&shy;
ing taken as meat.</xpln></wordentry>

</page>

<page sig="">

This Art was of great estimation among the ancient Grecians.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Golden number.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A num&shy;

teene, and then the gold&shy;
en number returneth to one again. For example, this yeere 1616. the gol&shy;
den number is two: the next yeer therefore it will be three, &amp;c. This golden number was devised to finde out the feast of Ea&shy;
ster.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Graces.</form> <xpln lang="en">A poeticall fic&shy;
tion of three Sisters, sup&shy;
posed the Daughters of <f type="i">Jupiter</f> and <f type="i">Venus.</f> They were called <f type="i">Aglaia, Thalia,</f> and <f type="i">Euphrosyne.</f> The mo&shy;
ralitie of this invention was, to expresse the mutual love and cheerefull con&shy;
versation which ought to be amongst friends: For they were painted naked, to signifie that friendship ought to be plain without dissimulation; smiling and  
</page>

<page sig="">
merry, to shew that men should do good willingly; young and maidenlike, to teach that friendship should consist in honest things; and holding hands together in a round ring, to shew that a benefit be&shy;
stowed, doth again return to the giver. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="'of(n)">Graines of Paradise.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A lit&shy;
tle seed, brought out of Armenia, of a strong sweet smell, and somewhat sharp in taste. It is hot and dry, of a subtill substance, and often used in Physicke. For it warmeth the inward parts, and is good against the falling sicknesse, the Sciatica, the Strangurie, bytings of venemous beasts, and divers other diseases.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Griffine.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A strange Bird in India, with foure feet armed with cruell clawes, being from the breast up&shy;
ward fashioned like an Ea&shy;
gle, but of purple colour, with red fierie eyes, and whitish wings, and in the hinder part blacke, made much like a Lion. This bird neasteth in the high mountaines, and is exceed&shy;
ing hard to be taken, ex&shy;
cept very yong; for he will adventure on any man, &amp; is so fierce, that he often killeth Elephants Dandra&shy;
gons. He is most enemy to horses: for which cause <f type="i">Virgil</f> in his eight Eclog, bringeth in the shepherd <f type="i">Damon,</f> who wondered at a strange marriage, speaking thus: <f type="i">Junguntur jam gry&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Habit</form> <xpln lang="en"> The outward at&shy;
tire of the body, whereby one person may be distin&shy;
guished from another; as the habit of a Gentleman, is different from the habit of a Merchant, and the ha&shy;
bit of a Handi-crafts man differing from them both. Sometime it signifieth a qualitie in the bodie or minde, not naturall, but gotten by long custome, or insused by God: as an O&shy;
rator still exercised in ma&shy;
king orations, hath gotten a habit of eloquent speak&shy;
ing; and the holy Apo&shy;
stles had a habit to under&shy;
stand and speak languages without studie.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Harpyes.</form> <xpln lang="en">A Poeticall word. It signifieth certaine monstrous birds, with mai&shy;

phalis</f>  a Lake of <f type="i">Arcadia,</f> and were sent by the Gods (if we beleeve Poets) to snatch away and defile  
</page>

<page sig="">
the meat of <f type="i">Phineus,</f> king of that countrey, because he at the perswasion of his second wife, had destroyed the children which he had by his first; they were at last driven away by <f type="i">Zetus,</f> and <f type="i">Calais</f> the sonnes of <f type="i">Boreas.</f> These Harpyes were named, <f type="i">Aello, Ocypete, Celeno,</f> and <f type="i">Thyella.</f>  </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Hermodactiles.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Little roots white, and round, sold by Apothecaries. They are hot and dry in the second degree, and are much used against all gouts, purging slimie fleame from farthest parts of the body: but they must be taken with Gin&shy;
ger, Anniseed or Cum&shy;
min, and a little Masticke, or else by stirring windi&shy;
nesse, they will overturne the stomacke.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Hierarchie.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The holy or&shy;
der of Angels, which con&shy;
taining nine degrees (as some affirme) is a mystical resemblance of the B. Tri&shy;
nitie, there being in nine, thrice three, and in every three thrice one. So that there are three superi&shy;
our, three inferiour, and three middle degrees. The superior are Seraphines, Cherubines, and Thrones; the middle, Dominati&shy;
ons, Principalities, Pow&shy;
ers: The inferior, ver&shy;
tues, Archangels, and An&shy;
gels.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Hieroglyphikes.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A darke mysticall kinde of writing, used chiefly in times past among the Pagane Priests and learned men of Egypt to hide their knowledge from the vulgar sort. This writing was by making the formes of beasts and di&shy;
vers other figures, and could hardly bee under&shy;
stood without exposition, or great knowledge in the nature of things. For ex&shy;
ample: Eternitie or everla&shy;
stingnesse, they expressed by a round circle, which hath no end: A king, by a scepter with an eye in the top thereof: A matter of haste, by a Dolphin, the swiftest of all fishes. And a matter of deliberation or advice, by an Anchor, which stayeth a ship in the waves. With a thousand such devices, not subject to common capacities to find out.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Homage.</form> <xpln lang="en">In Court Ba&shy;
rons, the Jury that are sworne to enquire of mat&shy;
ters, are so called. It signifi&shy;
eth also a servil ceremony, of duty by some Tenants to their Lords, after this manner. The Tenant that holdeth Lands by ho&shy;
mage, kneeling on both knees before the Lord (who sitteth and holdeth the Tenants hands be&shy;
tween his) saith as follow&shy;
eth. <f type="i">I become your man, from this day forward, of life and member, and of earthly honour, and to you shall be faithfull and true, and shall beare to you faith for the Lands I claime to hold of you, saving the faith that I owe to our Lord the King.</f> And then the Lord sitting must kisse the Tenant.  </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Hotchpot.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A terme in our common Law, when a daughter which hath had given unto her any lands to be coheire after in Franke marriage, clay&shy;

gether with the lands whereof her Father dyed seised, so that an equall di&shy;
vision may be made of the whole.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Hyena.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A beast like a Wolfe, having a mane and long haires over all the bodie. It is the subtillest (as some say) of all beasts, and will counterfeit the voice of a man, to draw sheep&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Hypocrite.</form> <xpln lang="en"> It properly signifieth one that doth counterfeit the gesture of an other man: but it is commonly taken for a dissembler, that with fained holines would seeme beter than he is indeed.</xpln></wordentry>

led by Apothecaries <f type="i">Hypo&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Hypostaticall.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Belonging to substance; or that which consisteth in the substance of a thing.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Hypotheticall.</form> <xpln lang="en"> In Logike, those propositions, which have a conjunction in them, and so consist of two parts, are called it <f type="i">Hypo&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Hysteron proteron.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A Greeke terme, sometime used in derision of that which is spoken or done preposterously or quite contrary. We call it in En&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Ides.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Eight daies in every moneth, so called, because they devide the moneth almost in the middle. In March, May, July, and October, these eight daies begin at the eighth day of the moneth, and conti&shy;
nue to the fifteenth: in o&shy;
ther moneths they begin at the sixt day, and continue to the thirteenth. Where note that the last day onely is called <f type="i">Ides,</f> and the first of these dayes, the eighth <f type="i">Ides,</f> the second the seventh <f type="i">Ides:</f> that is, the eigthth or seventh day before the <f type="i">Ides,</f> and so of the rest.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Incubus,</form> <xpln lang="en"> The night mare, when a man in his sleep supposeth he hath a great weight lying on him, and feeleth himselfe almost strangled; in such sort that he cannot turne him&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Irregularitie.</form> <xpln lang="en">A going out  
</page>

<page sig="">
of right rule. In the old Canon Law it is taken for any impediment, which hindereth a man from ta&shy;

riously defamed of any notable crime, or be may&shy;
med or much deformed, or have consented to procure anothers death, with di&shy;
vers other impediments too long to be reheatsed in this place. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Jubilation.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A great shout for joy, a great rejoycing.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Jubilie.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A publike rejoy&shy;
cing or a great shout for joy. Among the Hebrewes every fiftieth year, was cal&shy;
led the yeare of Jubilie; for then were bond-men of there owne Country made free, possessions returned againe to the first owners, neither was it lawfull to plant or sow any thing that yeare. Among Chri&shy;
stians this solemnitie of keeping a yeere of Jubilie, was first instituted by <f type="i">Bo&shy;
nifacius</f>  the eight, in the yeare of our Lord 1300. who ordained that it should be observed every hundreth yeare. After this <f type="i">Clement</f> the sixt, instituted it to be kept every fiftieth yeare. And lastly, <f type="i">Sixtus</f> the fourth brought it to be celebrated every five and twentieth yeare, be&shy;
ginning it first in the yeare of our Lord God, 1475.</xpln></wordentry>  
</page>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Iujubes.</form> <xpln lang="en">  Certain plumbs of Italy, sold here by A&shy;
pothecaries. This fruit is in colour white or red, in fashion round or like an olive, in tast sweet, having a hard long stone like an olive stone, but much lesse. If these plumbes be kept long, they waxe drie, and full of wrinckles. They are temperate in heate and cold, and are good against the cough, roughnesse of the throat, and against all exulcerations and inflam&shy;
mations of the kidnies and bladder: but being eaten for meate, they are of hard digestion.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Iulip.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A physicall cleere drinke made of distilled waters and sugar, used to coole the bodie, or quench thirst in hot diseases: some&shy;
times the <f type="i">Iuleps</f> have sirups or other mixtures put to them.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Iuncture.</form> <xpln lang="en">  A joynt, a joy&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Ivorie.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The Elephants tooth; it is of a binding nature, and the scraping thereof is good against sores, growing under the rootes of the nayles.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Labyrinth.</form> <xpln lang="en"> An intricate building or place made with so many turnings and windings, that whosoever went into it, could never get out without a perfect guide or a threed to direct him, the end of which threed must be tyed at the doore where he entreth. Some heathen Princes bestowed infinite charge, to build such devices in a stately forme. There were foure principall labyrinths in the world. The first in Egypt, made for a sepul&shy;
cher of one of their Kings, or (as some write) in ho&shy;
nour of the sunne. The se&shy;
cond in the Iland of Can&shy;
die, built at the comman&shy;
dement of King <f type="i">Minos,</f> by the ingenious workeman <f type="i">Daedalus,</f> who tooke his patterne, from that which he had seene in Egypt, the third in the Ile Lemnos, the fourth in Italy built by King <f type="i">Porsenna,</f> of great square stones, for his owne sepulcher.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Lacca.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A kinde of red gumme, brought out of Arabia, and sold here by Apothecaries, good a&shy;
gainst diseases of the breast, and comfortable to the Liver.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Laudanum.</form> <xpln lang="en">A yellowish gumme, as some write;  
</page>

<page sig="">
notwithstanding others af&shy;

taine herbe in Greece. <f type="i">A&shy;
vicen</f>  sayth, it is taken han&shy;
ging on the haire of Goats beards, that have fed upon that plant. It is hot and drie, and sweet of smell, if it be pure. It is often used in pomanders, and being annointed upon the head with oyle of myrtles, it doth strengthen the skin, and keepeth haire from falling off. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Lipothymie.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A fainting or swounding, when the vital spirits being suddenly op&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Liquid Amber.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A sweet Rosin brought from the West-Indies, comfortable to the braine, or any griefe proceeding from cold cau&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Lizard.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A little beast much like our Evet, but without poison, breeding in Italy &amp; other hot coun&shy;
tries. The dung of this beast is good to take away spots in the eye, &amp; cleereth the sight. And the head thereof being bruised and laid to, draweth out thorns, or any thing sticking with&shy;
in the flesh.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Litanies.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Prayers or sup&shy;
plications, so called of the Greeke verbe <f type="i">Litanevo,</f> which signifieth to beseech or intreat.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Litargie.</form> <xpln lang="en">The some that riseth from lead, when it is tried. It is cold of opera&shy;
tion, and often used by  
</page>

<page sig="">
Surgeons in Ointments and Plaisters, being of a gentle drying, cleansing, and binding nature. See <f type="i">Lethargie.</f> </xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Literature.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Learning: knowledge in books.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Litherly.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Slothfull.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Litigious.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Contentious, full of strife.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Liturgie.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Publike service of the Church.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Locall.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Of, or belonging to place.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Locust.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A great Flye or Grashopper with long legs, breeding in India, and other hot Countries. There are two kindes of them: One with wings, not good to be eaten, and the other without wings, which the inhabitants use for food.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">* Lodemanage.</form> <xpln lang="en">Skill of navigation.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Lodestar.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A Starre that guideth one.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Lodestone.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A stone of the colour of rusty iron, which hath an admirable vertue, not only to draw iron to it selfe, but also to make any iron, upon which it is rubbed, to draw iron also. It is written notwithstan&shy;
ding, that being rubbed with the juice of Gar&shy;
licke, it cannot then draw Iron, as likewise if a Dia&shy;
mond be laid close unto it. This stone is found in the Indian Sea, and also in the Countrey of Tra&shy;
chonitis: It is of greatest use in Navigation, for by it Sailers find out the certaine course of their voyage; the needle (in their compasse) tempered herewith, still standing di&shy;
rectly toward the North and South.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Logician.</form> <xpln lang="en"> One skilfull in Logicke.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Logicke.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The art of rea&shy;
son.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Lohoc.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A physicall word; It is a thicke sirupe or o&shy;
ther soft substance, which must not be swallowed, but suffered to melt of ie selfe in the mouth, that so it may gently slide downe, and thereby have the more vertue against diseases of the brest, lungs, and throat.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Lone.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Lending: sometime single or solitary.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Lyncuris.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A bright shining stone, and sometimes of a dark yellow color like Saf&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Mandrake.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A strange hearbe, bearing yellow round Apples. The root of this herbe is great and white like a Radish root, and is divided into two or moe parts, growing often almost like to the legs of a man. This root, espe&shy;
cially the barke thereof, is extreamely cold and dry, even to the fourth degree: It is therefore very dangerous to receive inwardly, for that the least quantity too much, will quickly kill one. Surge&shy;
ons use to steep this root in Wine, and give it to be drunken of such as they must cut, sawe, or burne in any part, for the cold operation thereof causeth sleep, and maketh the body insensible for a time.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Manna.</form> <xpln lang="en">In holy Scrip&shy;
ture it signifieth a delicate food, which God sent from heaven to the Israe&shy;
lites, in manner of a dew, white and somewhat like Coriander seed: with which the Israelites lived forty yeares in the Wil&shy;
dernesse, till they came to the borders of the land of Canaan. At the first  
</page>

<page sig="">
sending hereof, the people were in such admiration, that they said to each other <f type="i">Manbu? viz.</f> What is this? Which seemeth to be the cause, why it was after called Manna. In Physicke it is taken for a kind of dew, which fal&shy;
ling in hot Countries upon trees, doth there congeale, almost like to crums of white bread, and is gathered and choicely kept, as a gentle purger of choler. </xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Mansion.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A tarying or abiding: also a dwelling house.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Manumission.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A making one free from bondage.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Manumitte.</form> <xpln lang="en"> To make a slave or bondman free, which was in old time thus. The lord of the slave holding him by the head, arme, or other part, said before witnesse, I will that this man goe free, and in so saying, shoved him forward out of his hand.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Manure.</form> <xpln lang="en"> To till, to dresse land.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Marchasite.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A stone par&shy;
ticipating with the nature of some mettall, yet in so small quantity, that the mettall cannot be melted from it, but will vapour away in smoake, the stone turning to ashes. These Marchasites are common&shy;
ly in colour like to the mettall mixed with them; whether it be gold, silver, brasse, or any other. Some affirme a Marchasite to bee any stone out of which, fire may be stric&shy;
ken.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Margarites.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Little pearles found in the shell-fish, es&shy;
pecially in Orsters, where&shy;
of some have holes in them, and some have none. The best are brought out of India, yet they are also found in our English Seas, as also in the Fle&shy;
mish and Almaine, and the fairest Margarits grow in young shels. Some write that in thunder, the Oi&shy;
sters doe cast them up, as it were in way of aborti&shy;
on, which is the cause that they are often found in the sands. They are some&shy;
times used by Physitia~s in cordiall confections, for they comfort the spirits, and are therefore good a&shy;
gainst swoundings, having vertue also in them to stop issues of blood, or any loosenes of the bo&shy;
dy.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Mecoenas.</form> <xpln lang="en">It was the pro&shy;

mane, who being in great favour with <f type="i">Augustus</f> the Emperour, was a speciall friend to the Poets <f type="i">Virgill</f>  and <f type="i">Horacc,</f> and generally a supporter of all learned men: wherefore sometime  
</page>

<page sig="">
a great friend or patron is called a <f type="i">Mecoenas.</f> </xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Mechanichall.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Of or be&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Mechoacan.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A whitish root brought out of In&shy;
dia, called by some Indian or white Rubarbe. It is hot in the first, and dry in the second degree, and purgeth all humours of what kinde soever with much ease. It cleanseth and comforteth the liver and all the inward parts.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">* Mediate.</form> <xpln lang="en">To deale be&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Meninges.</form> <xpln lang="en">Thin skins in which the braine is contained. There are two such skins: one called by Physitians, <f type="i">Dura mater,</f> which is the stronger of the two, and next unto the scull. The other named <f type="i">Pia mater,</f> is within this first, being more tender and fine, and close wrapping the braine it selfe. If any of these skinnes bee  
</page>

<page sig="">
wounded, it causeth spee&shy;
dy death. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Metaphore.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The chang&shy;

ther sense like to it: as in saying, <f type="i">Covetousnesse is the roote of all evill:</f> Where the word Root, is called a Metaphore, because it sig&shy;
nifieth, the cause and be&shy;
ginning of all evill, even as a root is the cause, from whence a Plant spring&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Metaphoricall.</form> <xpln lang="en">  Spoken by a Metaphore.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Metaphysicks.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Arts which lifting themselves above the changeable nature of things, doe consider of such as do subsist in their own essence, not subject to any alteration! so that the Metaphysicks dealeth onely with incorporall, and everlasting things; and in this sense Schoole Divinitie is the highest part of the Metaphysicks, being chiefly occupied in contemplatory knowledge of God, angels, and souls of men.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Meteore.</form> <xpln lang="en">Any imper&shy;
fect mixt substance ingen&shy;
dred in the aire; as raine, snow, haile, thunder, lightning, blazing starres, cloudes, and winde: all which are made of va&shy;
pours  
</page>

<page sig="">
pours or exhalations drawn up from the earth and Sea, by attractive ver&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Microcosmus.</form> <xpln lang="en"> It proper&shy;

cosmus</f>  or little world, be&shy;
cause his body being com&shy;
pared to the baser part of the world, and his soule to the blessed Angels, seem&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Midriffe.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A sinewie skin passing overthwart in mans body, which divi&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Misy.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A kinde of yellow copperas, shining like gold, brought out of Egypt and the Ile of Cyprus: It is of a fretting burning nature, as the common Copperas is.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Mortuarie.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A payment due in some places for the buriall of the dead: which is foure shillings and foure pence, where the goods of the deceased partie are a&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Muske.</form> <xpln lang="en">It is brought out of India and other hote countries, taken there from a beast like a Roe, saving that it hath two crooked teeth bending inward like two hornes; within which beast the muske groweth, contained in a little bag or bladder. <page sig="">
conveyeth the vertues of diverse medicines, to those the two principall parts. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Myrobolanes.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A fruit growing in Egypt and Sy&shy;
ria, like Plummes, or Damsens. There are five kinds hereof, distingui&shy;
shed by these names, <f type="i">Citrinae, Indae, Cepulae, Emblicae,</f> and <f type="i">Belliricae.</f> They are cold in ope&shy;
ration, and comfortable to nature; The first of these purgeth choler, the second melancho&shy;
lie, and the three last fleame.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Myrrhe.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A Gumme brought out of Arabia, and Assyria, of colour between white and red, It is hot and dry in the second degree, or as some write in the third, and is often used in Phy&shy;
sicke, being of an ope&shy;
ning, cleansing, and dis&shy;
solving nature. Poets feine that Myrrhe, first came by reason of a kings daughter, named <f type="i">Myrrha,</f> who for a grievous crime committed, was by the Gods turned into a lit&shy;
tle Tree, out of the bran&shy;
ches whereof this Gumme still droppeth in man&shy;
ner of teares, as a token of her repentant sor&shy;
row.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Myrtle.</form> <xpln lang="en">A little low Tree growing in some hot countreyes, having small darke leaves, and bearing berries, which are of a binding nature, good to stop any issue of blood. This myrtle is a  
</page>

<page sig="">
tender plant, not able to endure cold, and was wont to be worne of the Ro&shy;
mane captaines garland-wise in triumph, when they had obtained any victory, without slaugh&shy;
ter of men: Poets con&shy;
secrated this Tree to <f type="i">Ve&shy;
nus.</f> </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Nazarite.</form> <xpln lang="en">In Hebrew it signifieth a man separa&shy;
ted or divided. The Jewes so called those that had vowed themselves for some time to God: And these Nazarites, while their vow lasted, were to abstaine from wine and Grapes, either greene or dry, and generally from all whatsoever came from the vine tree, or might make a man drunke. They also cut not their haire, but suffe&shy;
red it still to grow, and ob&shy;
served divers other cere&shy;
monies. The date of their vow being expired, they presented themselves to  
</page>

<page sig="">
the Priest, who offered a sacrifice for them; and then was the Nazarites haire cut, and burnt with the sacrifice. After which time it was lawfull, for the Nazarites to drinke wine as before. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Nones.</form> <xpln lang="en"> In March, May, Iuly, and October, they are the sixe dayes, next following the first day: In other moneths, they are the foure dayes next after the first; but the last of these dayes is properly cal&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Olibanum.</form> <xpln lang="en">The right frankincense, which is a Gumme, growing in Ara&shy;

ous  
</page>

<page sig="">
ayre, and to be put in plaisters for green wou~ds. The weight of two or three Wheat cornes swal&shy;

sume away in smoak. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Olympicke games.</form> <xpln lang="en">  So&shy;
lemne games of running, riding, wrastling, and other feats of activity, kept every fift yeare on a high hill in Greece, called Olympus. The reward to those that overcame in these trials, was nothing but a Garland of Olive branches, least covetous&shy;
nesse, rather than vertue should make men strive for victory: which was the cause that <f type="i">Tigranes</f> Sonne of <f type="i">Artabanus,</f> serving in the huge army of King <f type="i">Xerxes,</f> who then invaded Greece, said to <f type="i">Mardonius,</f> who had perswaded the King to that enterprize; against what a people have you brought us to fight, that more esteeme honour than money? Thinking it impossible to overcome such Nations as were so affected.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Opopanax.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A sap or liquor flowing in some hot coun&shy;
tries out of a Plant called <f type="i">Panax.</f> It is brought hither dry, being of a yellow co&shy;
lour on the outside, and white within, if it be not overstale. It is good a&shy;
gainst cold shiverings of agues, and to be drunke with meate, of such as are squat or bruised therein.</xpln></wordentry>
