<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Oracle.</form> <xpln lang="en"> An answer or counsel given by God. A&shy;
mong the Gentiles, these Oracles were but illusions of the devil, who answered for the most part doubt&shy;
fully in Idols, to questions or demands made unto him. There were two such principall places of Oracles: One of <f type="i">Ammon</f> or <f type="i">Iupiter,</f>  in Lybia, which was founded upon this oc&shy;
casion. When <f type="i">Bacchus</f> after his conquests in India, came into the hot burning countrey of Lybia, and wanted there water to re&shy;
leeve his Army, it is said that he prayed to his father <f type="i">Iupiter</f> to help him in that distresse: whereupon there appeared a Ramme unto him, which stamping upon the ground with his foot, caused a fountaine forth&shy;
with to spring in the place. <f type="i">Bacchus</f> obtaining by this meanes his desire, built a Temple there of excee&shy;
ding huge greatnesse, in the middle whereof, he erected an Idoll to <f type="i">Iupiter,</f>  in the likenesse of a Ram; which Idol afterward gave Oracles, and was called the Oracle of <f type="i">Ammon;</f> be&shy;
cause it was situated in a dry sandy place; for <f type="i">Am&shy;
mos</f> in Greeke signifieth Sand. The other Oracle was at Delphi, a Citie of Beotia in Greece, where <f type="i">Apollo</f> was said to give an&shy;
swers.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Oringos.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Certaine roots growing in some places, neere the Sea side. These roots are very long and deepe into the ground, of an ashie co&shy;
lour in the outside and full of joynts. They use, scra&shy;

gar or Honie, &amp; a litle Ci&shy;
namon and Ginger; which preserve is very good for a&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Palme.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The tree which beareth, Dates, growing plentifully in the holie land. There are of these trees found also in some parts of Egypt, but they beare no fruit, or if they beare any it is unpleasant. The branches of this tree, were wont to be carried as a token of victory, because they are of that nature, that they wil stil shoot upward, though oppressed with ne&shy;
ver so great weight, &amp; the leaves thereof never fall. Of this tree there is male and female: the male bea&shy;
reth only blossomes &amp; no fruit, but the female beares both. In old times, some people used to write with Paper, made of leaves of the Palme tree.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Panther.</form> <xpln lang="en">A fierce wilde  
</page>

<page sig="">
beast, having a sweet smell, and a faire spotted skin, wherewith she allureth o&shy;
ther beasts to looke on her; hiding her head lest it should make them afraid, and by this means, getteth her prey more easily. The male of this beast is the li&shy;
bard. The panthers (as is written) have on their shoulder a spot, which groweth and waineth like the Moon. This beast is so fearfull of the Hyena, that in his presence she dareth not doe any thing; in so much that if one have but a peece of the skinne of a Hyena, the Panther will not touch him, and it is said, that if both their skins be hanged together, the haire of the Panthers skin will fall off. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Paralipomenon.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Omitted, or not spoken of: There are two bookes in the old testament so call'd, because many worthy histories o&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Parallels.</form> <xpln lang="en">Lines running of an equall distance from each other, which can ne&shy;
ver meete, though they be drawne infinitely in length, thus,---.  
</page>

<page sig="">
In Astronomie there are five such imagined lines, running circlewise about the round com&shy;
passe of the heavens. The first is the Equinoctiall line just in the middle of the world betweene the two Poles. The second northward from the Equi&shy;
noctiall, is the Tropike of <f type="i">Cancer,</f> to which line the Sunne commeth about the twelfth day of June. The third (yet more northward) is the northern <img src="/images/illeg_lett_single.gif" width="5" height="9" border="0" alt="Single illegible letter" title="Single illegible letter">ircle, within 23. degrees and 50. minutes of the North-pole. The fourth line is the Tropike of <f type="i">Ca&shy;
pricorne,</f>  declining South&shy;
ward from the Equinocti&shy;
all, as much as the Tro&shy;
pike of <f type="i">Cancer</f> doth north&shy;
ward, and to this line the Sunne cometh about the twelfth of December. The fift and last line is the Southern circle, being as neere the South pole, as the Northerne circle (be&shy;
fore spoken of) is to the North pole. </xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Paralogisme.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A deceitfull syllogisme, a manner of arguing which seemeth true, when it is not: as in saying, <f type="i">He that affirmeth</f> William <f type="i">to be a living crea&shy;
ture, saith true.</f> <f type="i">He that affirmeth</f> William <f type="i">to be a Horse, affirmeth him to be a living creature.</f> <f type="i">Therefore he that affirmeth</f>  William <f type="i">to be a Horse saith true.</f> </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Phenix.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The rarest Bird in the world. It is written, that there was never any but one of this kind living at one time, and that only in Arabia; of the bignesse of an Eagle, of a purple colour, having a bright co&shy;
lour of gold about his necke, a goodly faire taile, and a tuft of feathers upon his head. He liveth above 600. yeares, and being old, buildeth him a nest of Cinnamon and the twigs of Frankincense, which he silleth with spices, and the~ with the labouring of his wings in the Sun, setting it on fire, is there consumed in it; out of whose Ashes there groweth a Worme, and of the worme another Phenix.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Philosophy.</form> <xpln lang="en">The study of wisedome: a deep know&shy;

1. <f type="i">Rationall</f>  Philosophy, in&shy;
cluding Grammer, Logick and Rhetoricke.

2. <f type="i">Naturall</f> Philosophy teaching the nature of all things, and containing besides Arith&shy;

3. <f type="i">Morall</f> Philosophy, which con&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Predicable.</form> <xpln lang="en">That which  
</page>

<page sig="">
may be reported or spoken of. In Logicke it signifi&shy;
eth certaine generall words, or universalities; whereof there are five, to wit, <f type="i">Genus, Species, Differen&shy;
cia, Proprium,</f> and <f type="i">Acci&shy;
dens.</f>  </xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Predicament.</form> <xpln lang="en">A terme of Logicke: It signifieth a different order in the na&shy;
ture of things, or certaine generall heads, to which they may be referred; and there are commo~ly recko&shy;
ned ten such predicaments.

The first called Substance, includeth all substances whatsoever, as the foure Elements, and all other creatures.

The second na&shy;
med Quantity, containeth all quantities, as ten, twen&shy;
ty, a yard, a furlong, a mile.

The third called Quality, hath under it all qualities, as wisedome, art, fortitude, dilegence, floth.

The fourth named Relatio~, is properly of such words, as depend mutually one upon ano&shy;
ther, as a husband and wife, a master and servant, a father and childe. The o&shy;
ther sixe are,

5. Action or doing: 

6. Passion or suffe&shy;
ring:  

7. Where: 

8. When: 

9. Situation or placing:

10. the Habit or outward co&shy;
vering of a thing.</xpln></wordentry>  

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Predicate.</form> <xpln lang="en"> To tell abroad, to report. In Logicke, it signifieth the later part of a proposition, as in saying, <f type="i">Paul</f> is an Apostle: The word Apostle is called pre&shy;
dicate, because it is spoken or affirmed of the subject <f type="i">Paul.</f></xpln></wordentry>

gree. The best of the Tree is the roote, next the boughes, then the body, but the principle good&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Sibyll.</form> <xpln lang="en">A woman inspi&shy;
red with aspirit of prophe&shy;
sie; so called of the greeke words <f type="i">Sios,</f> which signifi&shy;
eth God, and <f type="i">Boyle,</f> which signifieth counsell, because such women had know&shy;
ledge (as was said) of the counsell of God. There were ten <f type="i">Sibylls</f> famous a&shy;
bove the rest. The first was <f type="i">Sibylla</f> of Persia, the se&shy;
cond of Lybia, the third of the City Delphos in Greece, the fourth of Cuma, a city in Aeolis, the fift of Erythrae, a City of Asia, the sixt of the Ile, Sa&shy;
mos, the seventh of Cu&shy;
mae, a City of Campania in Italy. This <f type="i">Sibylla</f> of Cumae, (as it is written) came on a time to <f type="i">Tarquine</f>  King of Rome, in the ha&shy;
bit of a str~age old woman, offering to sell him nine bookes, full (as she said) of divine oracles; for which she demanded three hun&shy;
dred crownes of gold: the King not much regarding, and beside thinking them too deare at that price, she burned three of them be&shy;
fore his face, and then as&shy;
ked if he would have the other sixe, for which she demanded no lesse than she had don at first for the nin<img src="/images/illeg_lett_single.gif" width="5" height="9" border="0" alt="Single illegible letter" title="Single illegible letter">. Hereat the King de&shy;
riding  
</page>

<page sig="">
her and thinking her halfe mad, she burned three more of them, and told him that he should give her the same price for those three that were left. The King much wonder&shy;
ing at the womans con&shy;
stant earnestnesse, and therefore thinking the bookes contained no com&shy;
mon matter, commanded three hundred crownes to be given her for them, which she receiving, pre&shy;
sently vanished out of sight. These bookes were after kept by the Romans very carefully, who in great matters of doubt, alwayes had recourse to them, as to an assured ora&shy;
cle. The eighth <f type="i">Sibyll</f>  was called <f type="i">Sibylla</f> of Helespon&shy;
tus in Greece; the ninth was of Phrygia; the tenth and last of Tybur, a City neere Rome in Italy. All these <f type="i">Sibylls</f> prophecied of the incarnation of our Sa&shy;
viour Christ. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Sycomore.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A tree like a fig tree, having great bran&shy;
ches, and large leaves like a Mulberry. It beares fruit three or foure times in a yeare, much like a wilde Fig, but without any seeds within. The fruit groweth upon the very body of the tree, and the great maine boughes, and will never be ripe except it be scraped with an yron tool. It is found plentifully in Caria, Egypt, and the Ile of Rhodes; especially in such places, where Wheate will not grow.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Syllogisme.</form> <xpln lang="en">An Argu&shy;
ment consisting of three parts, whereby somthing is necessarily prooved, as thus: 

Every vertue is honorable: Patience is a vertue.

Therefore <f type="i">Patience is honourable.</f>

The first part of a syllo&shy;
gisme is called the Propo&shy;
sition or Major; the se&shy;
cond the Assumption or Minor; and the third, the Conclusion.</xpln></wordentry>

teenth day of the seventh moneth, and continued seven dayes, during which time the Israelites lived abroad in Tabernacles, in remembrance, that their Fathers a long time so li&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Talent.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A certaine value of money. Among the Greekes there were two kinds of talents, the grea&shy;
ter and the lesse: The greater contained about two hundred thirty three pounds sterling: the lesse about an hundred seventy five pounds. Among the Hebrewes the greater ta&shy;
lent of the Sanctuary con&shy;
tained 400. pounds, the lesser Talent halfe so much.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Tamariske.</form> <xpln lang="en">  A little tree bearing leaves not much unlike to heath; the de&shy;
coction whereof in Wine and a little Vineger be&shy;
ing drunken, is of great vertue against the hardnes or stopping of the spleene or Milt. This tree doth by nature so wast the Milt, that Swine which have been daily fed out of a vessell made thereof, have beene found to have no Milt at all.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Tropikes.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Two imagined circles in the spheare, of e&shy;

ther the Tropike of <f type="i">Capri&shy;
corne.</f> To the first the Sun cometh in June, to the o&shy;
ther in December. They are called Tropikes of the Greeke word <f type="i">Trepo,</f> which signifieth to turn, because when the Sunne comes to either of them, he turnes his course another way.</xpln></wordentry>

ward as other birds use. They live commonly eight yeers, and do breed twice a yeere, not above three egs at a time.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Vestals.</form> <xpln lang="en">Certaine virgins among the ancient Ro&shy;
manes, consecrated to the Goddesse <f type="i">Vesta.</f> They were alwayes chosen be&shy;
tweene sixe and tenne yeeres of age, and conti&shy;
nued thirtie yeeres in their office; whereof the  
</page>

<page sig="">
first ten yeeres they be&shy;
stowed in learning the ce&shy;
remonies of their order, the second they employed in execution thereof, and the last ten in teaching others, after it was law&shy;
full for them to marry if they would. Their chiefe office was to keepe fire continually burning in a round temple at Rome in honour of <f type="i">Ve&shy;
sta,</f>  and if it chanced to goe out, they were to renew it againe with no usuall fire, but such as they could get by art from the Sunne beames. They were greatly honoured in the Citie, and had diverse priviledges: for they were carried in Cha&shy;
riots, and the chiefest Magistrates would doe reverence to them. They had officers going before them, as the Consuls had, and if they met any who was ledde to be put to death, they had authori&shy;
tie to deliver him, taking an oath that they came not that way of purpose but by chance. They might also make a will, and dispose of their goods as they plea&shy;
sed. But if any of them were found to live unchast, shee was openly carried with sad silence to the gate called <f type="i">Collina,</f> where being put into a deepe pit, she was presently buried alive. These <f type="i">Vestals</f> were first in&shy;
stituted by <f type="i">Numa Pompili&shy;
us,</f> or as some write, by <f type="i">Romulus.</f>  </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Zone.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A girdle in Cos&shy;
mographic, it signifieth a division made of the hea&shy;
vens into five parts, where&shy;
of one is extreme hot, two extreame cold, and two temperate. The hot Zone, otherwise called the burn&shy;
ing Zone, is all that part of the heavens, which is contained betweene the two Tropickes of <f type="i">Cancer</f> and <f type="i">Capricorne,</f>  in which Zone, continually the Sun keepeth his course. The two cold Zones are under the two Poles of the world, or within 23 de&shy;
grees neere them. The two temperate are the Zones betweene the far&shy;
thest extreme cold and the middle burning Zone. And with these five Zones of the heavens, doth the earth under, agree in heat, cold temperature.</xpln></wordentry>
